<div id="review_186417548" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/186417548" />
    <a title="Mikela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4405952-mikela"><img alt="Mikela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1398731306p2/4405952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/186417548?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mikela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mikela" href="/user/show/4405952-mikela">Mikela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427986" href="/book/show/3265193-the-war-of-the-end-of-the-world">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4405952-mikela?shelf=1001-list-read">1001-list-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4405952-mikela?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4405952-mikela?shelf=peruvian-literature">peruvian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4405952-mikela?shelf=1900s">1900s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4405952-mikela?shelf=books-read-in-2012">books-read-in-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer186417548" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer46938703707030466">I find it very difficult to put into words my thoughts on this book. This is a historical novel based on the peasant revolt that took place in Brazil in the late 19th century when a renegade priest and his followers took over an abandoned estate in Canudos and established a community that refused to recognize the authority of the newly established republic, the old catholic church or civil marriages. Money was outlawed. There could be no taxation, census taking, marriage or ownership of property</span>
  <span id="freeText46938703707030466" style="display:none">I find it very difficult to put into words my thoughts on this book. This is a historical novel based on the peasant revolt that took place in Brazil in the late 19th century when a renegade priest and his followers took over an abandoned estate in Canudos and established a community that refused to recognize the authority of the newly established republic, the old catholic church or civil marriages. Money was outlawed. There could be no taxation, census taking, marriage or ownership of property in this new community. With the exception of former tax collectors, Freemasons or men who had worn the uniform of the republic all were welcomed, given a plot of land for their home and to farm. Thousands of former bandits, slaves, prostitutes, beggars and misfits of society flocked to Canudos where they were not only welcomed but made to feel human for the first time in many of their lives. This priest who they called The Counselor, talked to all with dignity and in the eyes of the peasants became a saint that they loved, protected and followed as he led.<br /><br />This was a time when the new republic was still on shaky footing with the large landowners, who were mostly Monarchists, still in opposition to the new government. Agents for the new government see Canudos as the perfect rallying cry for the country and accuse them of being agents of a foreign power in league with the Monarchists. As can be readily imagined, the government of the republic decided to wipe this community out before this type of revolt took root in other parts of Brazil and seriously undermined their central control. What followed was an epic battle where tens of thousands of republic soldiers fought the renegades of Canudos, losing thousands of their soldiers to a ragtag group of defenders of Canudos before finally prevailing.<br /><br />The story is told from the perspective of several points of view, residents of Canudos, believers and people with nothing making the pilgrimage to this haven, the landowners who were seeing their rich haciendas burned down and their workers leaving for Canudos, newspaper writers who followed the soldiers, government leaders and various other voices. We hear the resident of Canudos talk of their plans to defend themselves, to waylay supplies on their way to the republic soldiers, the co-operation of all and the love and respect they exhibited to one another. We hear from the soldiers about the humiliation of losing so many, the mutilations performed on the soldiers and the despair in the souls of the soldiers.<br /><br />This was an enormous undertaking and the writer to his credit wrote an epic masterpiece that left me in awe and wanting to find out more from this period of Brazil’s history. This is not a book for everyone, many will give up within the first 100 pages, but if you stay with it you’ll be rewarded richly. I highly recommend.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="46938703707030466" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143334444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10605253447180423019">Good God, this book is amazing. It has the massive cast and sprawl of War & Peace, and an apocalyptic feel that reminded me of - oh shut up - The Stand. It´s longer than it looks; Llosa has a terse style, so things happen at a rapid clip. It took me longer to read than I thought it would. But not like I was bored! This was one of those cases where I felt anxious as the end of the book neared, bummed out that it was going to be over soon.<br/><br/>It taught me that I need to pick books based on more than g</span>
  <span id="freeText10605253447180423019" style="display:none">Good God, this book is amazing. It has the massive cast and sprawl of War &amp; Peace, and an apocalyptic feel that reminded me of - oh shut up - The Stand. It´s longer than it looks; Llosa has a terse style, so things happen at a rapid clip. It took me longer to read than I thought it would. But not like I was bored! This was one of those cases where I felt anxious as the end of the book neared, bummed out that it was going to be over soon.<br /><br />It taught me that I need to pick books based on more than geographical location. I thought it would be fun to read a Latin American author while on vacation in Mexico, but reading these epic scenes of slaughter and carnage in front of a pool with men in starched uniforms offering me daquiris and Bob Marley blaring tinnily behind me felt frankly gross.<br /><br />Which reminds me, I should warn you: this isn´t for the faint of heart. The final 300 pages or so are absolutely nothing but death. Also: for several reasons, do not make a drinking game where you have to do a shot every time someone gets his penis cut off and shoved into his mouth.<br /><br />Postscript: I've done some research; the book is almost entirely true. It's about the War of Canudos in the late 19th century in Brazil - so that's just around when HG Wells was writing, just before Edith Wharton, for perspective. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10605253447180423019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1904976546" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1904976546" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12933866-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444136022p2/12933866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1904976546?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/17449222-the-war-of-the-end-of-the-world">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=nobel-winners">nobel-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1904976546" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2011530444996175155">Despite its epic feel, fantastically evocative prose, mythic themes and strange, archetypal characters, I found little to propel me with much enthusiasm through this novel. The narrative is extremely granular, which is not necessarily a bad thing, but when one is not engaged in the detail of the plot or characters, it can become quite tedious. The story is full of symbolism, with certain characters directly evoking obvious archetypes, and though this helped establish the novel’s mythical atmosph</span>
  <span id="freeText2011530444996175155" style="display:none">Despite its epic feel, fantastically evocative prose, mythic themes and strange, archetypal characters, I found little to propel me with much enthusiasm through this novel. The narrative is extremely granular, which is not necessarily a bad thing, but when one is not engaged in the detail of the plot or characters, it can become quite tedious. The story is full of symbolism, with certain characters directly evoking obvious archetypes, and though this helped establish the novel’s mythical atmosphere, I ultimately found the comparisons (and often the characters themselves) to be a little too shallow and a little too blatant, traits which were often exposed by the level of narrative detail. <br /><br />As its driving force, the novel seems to rely on the urgency and drama of the war itself – something that unfortunately I never came to feel through these pages. Too often it just felt like a sequence of minor events; one small thing of no consequence after another (I admit to reading the later battle scenes much less attentively than I could have). Reading through other reviews, it appears that what I found to be the novel’s weaknesses, others have seen as strengths. What can I say? Who really knows why we enjoy one thing and not another? Sometimes there is a feeling, and other times that feeling is not there. Perhaps that’s really all there is to it, and the rest is just rationalisation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2011530444996175155" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

A medieval meets the wild west landscape(turn of the century Brazil) of prophets, bandits, water witches, droughts, a storytelling dwarf, flagellants, miracle healers, madmen, plagues, vultures, rats, a revolutionary phrenologist, pariah dogs, Barons whose time has past, circus freaks, a u</span>
  <span id="freeText11647550697053224606" style="display:none">Another tale of mayhem, history and the macabre from Vargas Llosa. Whereas Death in the Andes was compared to a Diane Arbus styled portrait the visual artists this book evokes is Heiryonmous Bosch or Breughal. A medieval meets the wild west landscape(turn of the century Brazil) of prophets, bandits, water witches, droughts, a storytelling dwarf, flagellants, miracle healers, madmen, plagues, vultures, rats, a revolutionary phrenologist, pariah dogs, Barons whose time has past, circus freaks, a utopian colony, and marauding destructive army. Festooned with grotesques and corpses this is a book of violence and horror but it is a conflation of real history and not fiction. A historical novel in the tradition of Tolstoy, McCarthy, and Lampedusa. Exhaustingly long but worth it.</span>

<div id="review_1347886621" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1347886621" />
    <a title="Bryan Murphy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10860999-bryan-murphy"><img alt="Bryan Murphy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442635328p2/10860999.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1347886621?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryan Murphy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryan Murphy" href="/user/show/10860999-bryan-murphy">Bryan Murphy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1347886621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1857838553368640411">This novel reminds me forcefully of Conor Cruise O'Brien's play "Murderous Angels" about men in thrall to conflicting ideals that lead them to disaster. O'Brien's play was written in the 1960s and set in the 1960s. Vargas Llosa wrote his novel at the turn of the millennium but set it in the 1890s. The ideals that O’Brien focused on (the "Murderous Angels" in people’s heads) thus seem more contemporary: freedom and peace, whereas Vargas Llosa sets religious fundamentalism against nationalism, ide</span>
  <span id="freeText1857838553368640411" style="display:none">This novel reminds me forcefully of Conor Cruise O'Brien's play "Murderous Angels" about men in thrall to conflicting ideals that lead them to disaster. O'Brien's play was written in the 1960s and set in the 1960s. Vargas Llosa wrote his novel at the turn of the millennium but set it in the 1890s. The ideals that O’Brien focused on (the "Murderous Angels" in people’s heads) thus seem more contemporary: freedom and peace, whereas Vargas Llosa sets religious fundamentalism against nationalism, ideals which might seem less likely in our supposedly rational days to set people murdering each other and destroying their own lives, were they not already doing so in the Middle East and no doubt heading our way. Vargas Llosa’s great achievement here is to present events from the points of view of individuals with a range of irreconcilable beliefs and attitudes, and to do so without condescension or sentimentality, so that the reader can feel compassion for the characters, if not quite empathy. The author is a gifted story-teller, but is perhaps too wrapped up in that talent, for he drags the story on too long, so that by the time are supposed to feel the most compassion, compassion fatigue has begun to set in.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1857838553368640411" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1098437" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer3609281989101814042">This is a book I ought to have picked up way back when I was a teen-ager and could stomach these excessively long-winded South American novels. Don't get me wrong...I found the book sort of fascinating until about the last 150 pages. And then I realized it was just going to go on in the same circles until it ran out of steam. And then, folks, I skipped to the final five pages and read those. The last paragraph is worth it. Those 145 pages I skipped, I find it difficult to believe I've missed any</span>
  <span id="freeText3609281989101814042" style="display:none">This is a book I ought to have picked up way back when I was a teen-ager and could stomach these excessively long-winded South American novels. Don't get me wrong...I found the book sort of fascinating until about the last 150 pages. And then I realized it was just going to go on in the same circles until it ran out of steam. And then, folks, I skipped to the final five pages and read those. The last paragraph is worth it. Those 145 pages I skipped, I find it difficult to believe I've missed anything substantial. (I did skim them, rapidly)<br /><br />Ok. Turn of the century Brazil. The major players are the Republicans (divided into a militaristic and a parliamentarian grouping), the Monarchists (landowners) and an Anarchist-type on the run (aren't they always) who finds a certain (somewhat deluded) affinity with a group of religious folk who have decided (or who have chosen to accept as doctrine from one charismatic leader) to fight the Republic and all of its godless ways while at the same time living out very practically the idealist communal style the Anarchist has been fighting for.<br /><br />And they all fight each other in a series of battles. There are the usual intrigues of war, rapes, political philosophies being bantered about, starving conscripted soldiers and glorified religious figures...<br /><br />I did enjoy it, mostly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3609281989101814042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1401646764?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/18954745-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427986" href="/book/show/3265193-the-war-of-the-end-of-the-world">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1401646764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10276582246162975471">The story of a doomed catholic uprising in the sertao in 19th century NE Brazil. Flocking to the town of Canudos, drawn by the magnetism of the ascetic wandering figure known as the Counselor, a mishmash of freaks, murderers, bandits, mystics, communists and otherwise downtrodden masess begin an uprising against the newly formed Brazilian republic. Fearing the republics secular posture, the religious fanatics call for a return of the monarchy and prepare for the inevitable confrontation with the</span>
  <span id="freeText10276582246162975471" style="display:none">The story of a doomed catholic uprising in the sertao in 19th century NE Brazil. Flocking to the town of Canudos, drawn by the magnetism of the ascetic wandering figure known as the Counselor, a mishmash of freaks, murderers, bandits, mystics, communists and otherwise downtrodden masess begin an uprising against the newly formed Brazilian republic. Fearing the republics secular posture, the religious fanatics call for a return of the monarchy and prepare for the inevitable confrontation with the armies sent to pacify them.<br /><br />This book captures the feel of the sertao and its ignorant, superstitious, savage and proud inhabitants. Full of fantastic characters like Pajeu, Satan Joao, the little blessed one, Galileo Gall, the Lion of Natuba, and the hard nosed army colonel sent to defeat them. When asked why they didn't flee the oncoming army, the residents of Canudos replied, they already had run, to Canudos, and there was nowhere left to run to. The battles in the deserts of northern Brasil are brutal and savage as the peasants fight for their lives and souls against the troops of the heathen republic.<br /><br />My only concern with this book was how dry it read and I fear that perhaps a better translation might be available somewhere. Seems like something is a little off. Anyways, really great to read a story based in Bahia and I enjoyed it immensely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10276582246162975471" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_548729980" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/548729980" />
    <a title="Robbie Bruens" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4316993-robbie-bruens"><img alt="Robbie Bruens" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348966919p2/4316993.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/548729980?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robbie Bruens" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robbie Bruens" href="/user/show/4316993-robbie-bruens">Robbie Bruens</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/17449222-the-war-of-the-end-of-the-world">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4316993-robbie-bruens?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4316993-robbie-bruens?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4316993-robbie-bruens?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4316993-robbie-bruens?shelf=translations">translations</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4316993-robbie-bruens?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4316993-robbie-bruens?shelf=war">war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer548729980" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13306306100588385965">Nobel Laureate Vargas Llosa recounts the epic story of the Canudos rebellion in fin-de-siècle Brazil in an interminable novel that resembles an impeccably detailed outline for a costume drama miniseries. There are so many characters and settings that the book occasionally feels like studying for an exam. As someone with a bachelors' degree in history and a longtime intense fascination with tales of the past, I would imagine I am exactly the target audience for this type of book. Alas, while ther</span>
  <span id="freeText13306306100588385965" style="display:none">Nobel Laureate Vargas Llosa recounts the epic story of the Canudos rebellion in fin-de-siècle Brazil in an interminable novel that resembles an impeccably detailed outline for a costume drama miniseries. There are so many characters and settings that the book occasionally feels like studying for an exam. As someone with a bachelors' degree in history and a longtime intense fascination with tales of the past, I would imagine I am exactly the target audience for this type of book. Alas, while there are many dozens of indelible passages to be found here, the sum of this novel's innumerable parts add up to a curiously sprawling yet dull expanse. Perhaps the characters bleed together too readily, or Vargas Llosa's propensity for describing events from many different perspectives just doesn't reveal much in the way of new insights but rather feels repetitious. Given how long I have spent with this book (nearly a year, as I began reading it when I was actually traveling in Vargas Llosa's home country of Peru last year), I could probably expound for quite some time on what didn't work very well, as well as the book's many pleasures. Instead, I'll just say that The Time of the Hero is an excellent book with all the emotional depth and structural sophistication that this book lacked. I hope that when I read more of Vargas Llosa's books in the future, they hew closer to the febrile genius of The Time of the Hero and less to the wasted genius of The War of the End of the World.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13306306100588385965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_318681983" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/318681983" />
    <a title="Mosca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1837675-mosca"><img alt="Mosca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363662463p2/1837675.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/318681983?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mosca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mosca" href="/user/show/1837675-mosca">Mosca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140262605" href="/book/show/293910.The_War_of_the_End_of_the_World">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mosca by: </span>
      I stole this book from JSou
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1837675-mosca?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer318681983" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14143002310747051465">-----------------------------<br/><br/>This book is not for everyone. If you are easily offended, or your stomach is weak; you will not like this book. <br/><br/>But, having said that, I can only describe this book as a masterpiece--that is not easily described.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/293910.The_War_of_the_End_of_the_World' rel='nofollow noopener'>The War of the End of the World</a> is a work of historical fiction, set among particular events that occurred in the very late nineteenth century in northeastern Brazil. Wikipedia provides a simplified background <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_of_Canudos' rel='nofollow noopener'>Here</a>. This background is sketchy. But I have fou</span>
  <span id="freeText14143002310747051465" style="display:none">-----------------------------<br /><br />This book is not for everyone. If you are easily offended, or your stomach is weak; you will not like this book. <br /><br />But, having said that, I can only describe this book as a masterpiece--that is not easily described.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/293910.The_War_of_the_End_of_the_World" rel="nofollow noopener">The War of the End of the World</a> is a work of historical fiction, set among particular events that occurred in the very late nineteenth century in northeastern Brazil. Wikipedia provides a simplified background <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_of_Canudos" rel="nofollow noopener">Here</a>. This background is sketchy. But I have found it very, very difficult to get related and historically-informative background in English. (This is a result of a cultural, self-inflicted, arrogant disability infecting us gringos, IMHO.) This information fog has made it difficult for me to separate the actual historical events from those fictionalized by the author. So I am assuming that the larger-scale contextual events are actual; while many of the more immediate characters and character traits are fictionalized.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/293910.The_War_of_the_End_of_the_World" rel="nofollow noopener">The War of the End of the World</a> is an unflinching and very painful retelling of an immense historical tragedy. The fictionalized and historical experiences of the important historical characters, and the fictional characters, are very immediate--and frequently amoral. But these characterizations are so well conceived and described that I, at least, found myself sympathizing with many people whose actions I would later find appalling.<br /><br />These characters are numerous, frequently grotesque, sometimes despicable, sometimes tragic, sometimes heroic, and always flesh-and-blood human. The reader finds these characters within a sprawling narrative that transports us across the landscapes and cityscapes of nineteenth century Bahia, Brazil. The historical context that they all share irreversibly drives them, and the reader, towards the inevitable cataclysm.<br /><br />For me, the importance of the actual historical story, the tenaciously powerful narrative style, and the countless tragically-flawed characters are what makes this experience in re-created history so emotionally moving and so devastating.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22515.Mario_Vargas_Llosa" title="Mario Vargas Llosa" rel="nofollow noopener">Mario Vargas Llosa</a> does not flinch once describing both with loving intimacy or in blunt horror all of the necessary elements of this unforgettable story. This is the first book of his that I have read. It will not be the last.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14143002310747051465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_423288078" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/423288078" />
    <a title="Paul Gleason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8505940-paul-gleason"><img alt="Paul Gleason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417471039p2/8505940.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/423288078?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Gleason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Gleason" href="/user/show/8505940-paul-gleason">Paul Gleason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer423288078" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16501380560222002728">Vargas Llosa's The War at the End of the World reminds me of the second movement of Beethoven's Seventh Symphony in its majestic beauty, depth, solemnity, and sense of imposing doom. It's a relentless book - one that's meant to be savored and learned from.<br/><br/>Vargas Llosa's inspiration was the Brazilian government's military campaign to obliterate Canudos, a modern-day utopia. The text is really about the conflict between secularism and religion that developed in Brazil in the nineteenth century. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText16501380560222002728" style="display:none">Vargas Llosa's The War at the End of the World reminds me of the second movement of Beethoven's Seventh Symphony in its majestic beauty, depth, solemnity, and sense of imposing doom. It's a relentless book - one that's meant to be savored and learned from.<br /><br />Vargas Llosa's inspiration was the Brazilian government's military campaign to obliterate Canudos, a modern-day utopia. The text is really about the conflict between secularism and religion that developed in Brazil in the nineteenth century. This struggle also has political overtones: the founders of the village at Canudos have nothing, while the military and pro-military folk have everything, including most of the country's land. At first, the military thinks that the village is harmless, but then its leaders reach the slow realization that something of a revolution is at foot - and the revolution must be stopped.<br /><br />I'm not going to spoil what happens in the book, but you've probably already guessed which side wins. <br /><br />Most critics argue - and I agree - that Vargas Llosa's true triumph in the book is his postmodern employment of disparate literary styles to create his characters and their inner lives. These styles include the eighteenth-century picaresque to describe the "rational" inner life of a particular character, as well as modernist interior monologue - or whatever it is that Faulkner is really up to. <br /><br />Side note: It's totally amazing to me still that Faulkner has such a pervasive influence on the Latin American novel and García Márquez, Vargas Llosa, and Bolano, the three greatest LA novelists.<br /><br />The book also owes a lot to the Greeks. A myopic journalist who loses his glasses becomes a Tiresias of sorts. He's not a prophet, but a gatherer of information - the only character in the novel who has a shot of making sense of the economic, class, religious, and violent events that populate Vargas Llosa's text. Through him we have a fleeting glimpse of what actually happens - or will happen - at the war at the end of the world.<br /><br />Vargas Llosa, in conclusion, is himself a prophet. The journalist could be a stand-in for him. Both the novelist's and character's conclusions seem only too appropriate for our time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16501380560222002728" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1943306476" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1943306476" />
    <a title="Kevin Tole" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5884970-kevin-tole"><img alt="Kevin Tole" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571350292p2/5884970.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1943306476?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Tole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Tole" href="/user/show/5884970-kevin-tole">Kevin Tole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5884970-kevin-tole?shelf=conflict">conflict</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5884970-kevin-tole?shelf=anthropology">anthropology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5884970-kevin-tole?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5884970-kevin-tole?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5884970-kevin-tole?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5884970-kevin-tole?shelf=politics">politics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1943306476" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11379312580066119169">The book is to a greater extent an examination by a Peruvian writer of events that happened in Brazil at the end of the 19th century at Canudos in Bahia State. The time is important as the old century moved towards the start of the new and the millenarians began to appear to warn of the coming of a Great Change with the end of 1000 years. This is the story of the Counsellor who begins to appear in the <i>sertão</i>, the back country inland from Bahia. He challenges the peons lack and loss of faith in t</span>
  <span id="freeText11379312580066119169" style="display:none">The book is to a greater extent an examination by a Peruvian writer of events that happened in Brazil at the end of the 19th century at Canudos in Bahia State. The time is important as the old century moved towards the start of the new and the millenarians began to appear to warn of the coming of a Great Change with the end of 1000 years. This is the story of the Counsellor who begins to appear in the <i>sertão</i>, the back country inland from Bahia. He challenges the peons lack and loss of faith in the Church as he seeks to change their attitudes and beliefs by personal action. He teaches of the coming of The Dog, and within that rails against the political stance seen by the Republic. The Republic has freed the black slaves in Brazil but it is the moves by the Republicans to abolish the sacred and move to the secular that makes the Counsellor rail against it. Gradually he attracts followers in the lawless back country and as they gather numbers they eventually become a force. <br /><br />This force, whilst still having a core of Catholic religious mystique (and mystic) and asceticism, is used in a political way firstly against the owners of the haciendas in the back country who are all at their core monarchists and yearn for the fall of the Republic so that they can regain the power they had as the government of the people by their betters. It is like old time patrician Conservatives aiming to do best for their country and ‘the People’ whilst denying them democratic rights to rule themselves. And gradually The Counsellor and his followers, who are at heart peaceful and wish to see a return to the sacred and to lose the degradation of what has happened to them as pawns in the never-ending grind of political movement through history, themselves become pawns again, used for different ends by both the Monarchists and the Republicans. And so follows the War to dispose of these heretics that can be seen from the one side as wanting to end the power of the Republic and on the other to attack the fascistic power of the supporters of Monarchy. As such, at the heart of this novel is a deeply political message. <br /><br />It was not, however, till over 500 pages through this epic of a book that a clear idea of what the writer was trying to say starts to emerge. By that time most of the narrative has unfolded. The main characters have all come along and been fleshed out with back stories and some have been killed off. But what becomes dominant is a theme of 'disillusionment'.<br /><br />My initial reflections as I read the book were all to do with the tie up's with the religious-political movements which abounded at the end of the English Revolutionary Civil War with the defeat of the Royalist forces, the beheading and banishment of monarchy and the establishment of the Commonwealth of the English Parliament. Those that had been predominantly fighting for parliamentary forces against the royalists began to call for an openness and tolerance, for recognition of the equality and fraternity of all men and women. And this gave rise to the movements of Ranters, Levellers, Quakers, Shakers, and Diggers who all in their way made political statements couched sometimes within religious beliefs. They were all either expelled or dealt with to such a degree that they never raised their heads again as a political force for change.<br /><br />The Counsellor and his followers are like the millenarians of the English Civil War, and like the foot soldiers of Cromwell's army they are used as tools to enforce political power by counter-revolutionary forces. The forces that use them care not in the least what the message of the Counsellor’s followers is. The Canudos rebels stand in defiance of both the Republicans who have the power and the Landowner Monarchists. It is through this theme of misuse and misunderstanding that the main theme of disillusionment plays out. Because they can be seen as revolutionaries, the Canudos rebels <u> MUST</u> be stamped out.<br /><br />Mario Vargas Llosa writes wonderfully if at length to play out these forces. The methodology is dramatic and theatric, even cinematic. You could see this book easily becoming a film. Events are played out sometimes in flashbacks or as narratives from individuals. Characters are fleshed out with back stories in interludes within the line of the narrative. For me this did not break up the theme but gave body to it. There is more narrative here with Vargas Llosa than say the typical example of Latin American <i>‘magical realism’</i>. There is less ‘magic’ and more ‘realism’ and distinctly more narrative than say Marquez, who Vargas Llosa publicly fell out with after writing his doctoral thesis on Marquez, or for that matter the daddy of them all in Borges. It is not difficult to see why Vargas Llosa went on to become a political candidate in Peru and to run for the Presidency, ultimately failing to win the election in 1990. His own personal politics moved from early left wing socialism in his youth to liberal right wing conservatism. Therefore, that at the core of this novel should be the notion of disillusionment is in many ways surprising. Here is a writer that crossed the political floor from left to right but still believed in democratic political structure whilst retaining in his own personal beliefs elements of libertarianism and anti-nationalism. Perhaps then the message within is not necessarily about disillusionment but about the inability of one side to see the other and is therefore a call for tolerance. Whatever the message this is a great novel and probably on the basis of this book, pushed the Swedish Committee to award him the Nobel Prize for Literature in 2010, 29 years after The War of the End of the World was published, for his <i>"for his cartography of structures of power and his trenchant images of the individual's resistance, revolt, and defeat."</i>. <br /><br />It is a novel that should be read and a brick in the wall of great world literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11379312580066119169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('d1ut5Ytc8j9+RRPM+Kp/3m6Ajcxoj0ZBH/YcPyDFawlL7VnJNkgfYDGAbwCTHkT5Li60Eg4GiSlJEUPo6yuPRw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

A violent showdown in the <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1046831312877095232\" style=\"display:none\">New Year’s Eve, 1975: Arturo Belano and Ulises Lima, founders of the visceral realist movement in poetry, leave Mexico City in a borrowed white Impala. Their quest: to track down the obscure, vanished poet Cesárea Tinajero. A violent showdown in the Sonora desert turns search to flight; twenty years later Belano and Lima are still on the run.\n\nThe explosive first long work by “the most exciting writer to come from south of the Rio Grande in a long time” (Ilan Stavans, Los Angeles Times), The Savage Detectives follows Belano and Lima through the eyes of the people whose paths they cross in Central America, Europe, Israel, and West Africa. This chorus includes the muses of visceral realism, the beautiful Font sisters; their father, an architect interned in a Mexico City asylum; a sensitive young follower of Octavio Paz; a foul-mouthed American graduate student; a French girl with a taste for the Marquis de Sade; the great-granddaughter of Leon Trotsky; a Chilean stowaway with a mystical gift for numbers; the anorexic heiress to a Mexican underwear empire; an Argentinian photojournalist in Angola; and assorted hangers-on, detractors, critics, lovers, employers, vagabonds, real-life literary figures, and random acquaintances.\n\nA polymathic descendant of Borges and Pynchon, Roberto Bolaño traces the hidden connection between literature and violence in a world where national boundaries are fluid and death lurks in the shadow of the avant-garde. The Savage Detectives is a dazzling original, the first great Latin American novel of the twenty-first century.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1046831312877095232\" href=\"#\" violentos nos llega suavemente de la mano de Ciriaco, un chico tenaz, hiperactivo y enérgico de catorce años quien luego de jugarle un par travesuras a las personas equivocadas descubrirá que la       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/22515.Mario_Vargas_Llosa">About Mario Vargas Llosa</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/22515.Mario_Vargas_Llosa"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1569483424p3/22515.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/22515.Mario_Vargas_Llosa">Mario Vargas Llosa
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
6,205
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13462839181017144510">Mario Vargas Llosa, born in Peru in 1936, is the author of some of the most significant writing to come out of South America in the past fifty years. His novels include <i>The Green House</i>, about a brothel in a Peruvian town that brings together the innocent and the corrupt; <i>The Feast of the Goat</i>, a vivid re-creation of the Dominican Republic during the final days of General Rafael Trujillo’s insidiou</span>
  <span id="freeText13462839181017144510" style="display:none">Mario Vargas Llosa, born in Peru in 1936, is the author of some of the most significant writing to come out of South America in the past fifty years. His novels include <i>The Green House</i>, about a brothel in a Peruvian town that brings together the innocent and the corrupt; <i>The Feast of the Goat</i>, a vivid re-creation of the Dominican Republic during the final days of General Rafael Trujillo’s insidious regime; and <i>Aunt Julia and the Scriptwriter</i>, a comedic semi-autobiographical account of an aspiring writer named Marito Varguitas, who falls in love with Julia, the divorced sister-in-law of his Uncle Lucho. <br /><br />He is also a widely read and respected essayist, writing everything from newspaper opinion pieces to critical works on other writers, including <i>The Perpetual Orgy</i> on Flaubert.<br /><br />Vargas Llosa is also active outside the literary arena, and was a serious contender for the presidency of Peru in 1990 (eventually losing to the now disgraced Alberto Fujimori), an experience he documented in his memoir, <i>A Fish in the Water</i>. <br /><br />On the controversial nature of some of his work he said, “The writer’s job is to write with rigor, with commitment, to defend what they believe with all the talent they have. I think that’s part of the moral obligation of a writer, which cannot be only purely artistic. I think a writer has some kind of responsibility at least to participate in the civic debate. I think literature is impoverished, if it becomes cut from the main agenda of people, of society, of life.” <br /><br />He was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature for the year 2010, "for his cartography of structures of power &amp; his trenchant images of the individual's resistance, revolt, and defeat".<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://us.macmillan.com/author/mariovargasllosa">http://us.macmillan.com/author/mariov...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13462839181017144510" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
